Commentary
This passage is a theological-philosophical discussion about the nature of perception (idrak) and knowledge (ilm), particularly as applied to God's attributes. The author begins by arguing that one can distinguish between knowledge and perception based on their different loci and conditions. He notes that visual perception requires facing and confrontation between the perceiver and the perceived, while auditory perception does not. Smell, taste, and touch require physical contact of bodies, but hearing and sight do not. On this basis, it is permissible to describe God as Hearing and Seeing, but not as Smelling, Tasting, or Touching, since the latter would imply physical contact which is impossible for God. This reasoning shows that perceptions are diverse in both their realities and their loci. This conclusion follows for one who distinguishes between perception and knowledge. If one instead holds that perception is knowledge, then the locus and reality are unified. The author then presents the specific view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who holds that every perception is knowledge (on one opinion) but not every knowledge is perception. For al-Ash'ari, perception is a specific kind of knowledge that requires determination of the perceived object and relates only to existent things; existence itself is what makes perception valid (musahhih). In contrast, absolute knowledge (al-ilm al-mutlaq) does not require determination of the perceived object, nor does it relate only to the existent; it can relate to the non-existent, existent, necessary, contingent, and impossible. Among perceptions, hearing and sight are specific kinds of knowledge. Al-Ash'ari was uncertain about the other senses (smell, taste, touch) — whether they are specific sciences or merely perceptions. Some of his followers considered them to be other perceptions, and they held that mere existence is not sufficient for them; rather, contact is a condition. The author then explains that smell cannot be conceived without contact of particles from the air (or vapor) to the olfactory organ, and similarly taste and touch require contact of one body with another. Finally, the author mentions the philosophers' view that an image (surah) is imprinted on the external senses through forms that emanate upon them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if perception is knowledge, then all knowledge must be perception (i.e., knowing something requires sensing it).
Correction: Al-Ash'ari clarifies that perception is only a subset of knowledge; absolute knowledge is much broader and does not require existence or sensory contact.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, what distinguishes perception from absolute knowledge?
A) Perception requires the object to exist and be specified.
B) Perception is limited to hearing and sight only.
C) Absolute knowledge only relates to necessary things.
D) Perception requires physical contact with the object.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari defines perception as a specific kind of knowledge that requires the existence and specification of the object, unlike absolute knowledge which is broader.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you more driven by what you perceive with your senses (like physical blessings or trials) than by the certain knowledge of unseen realities—like Paradise, Hell, and the Day of Judgment? How does this imbalance reflect in your daily choices?
?
Al-Ash'ari says perception only relates to what exists. Think of one moment today when you acted based on a sensory perception rather than on a truth you know by faith. What would change if you prioritized absolute knowledge over perception in that situation?
?
If absolute knowledge can relate to impossible things, such as the fact that Allah has no partner or that He cannot be unjust, does that knowledge actually govern your thoughts when you face a situation that seems unfair? Challenge yourself to pinpoint one area where your actions contradict what you claim to know.

Setting the Framework on Ru'ya
Sets up the disagreement on ru'ya and the two definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the background debate on ru'ya (seeing God) and the two main interpretations of what ru'ya is.

Core Idea
The debate on ru'ya hinges on whether it is a distinct perception or a form of knowledge.

The passage begins by clarifying what ru'ya is not: no Muslim theologian holds a physicalist view. The real debate is over the nature of ru'ya itself—whether it is a perception beyond knowledge or a specific type of knowledge. This lesson sets up the framework for the Ash'ari proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening paragraph
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's initial clarification that no one from Ahl al-Qibla accepts physicalist theories of vision.
2
Identify the two views on ru'ya
The passage states the disagreement: is ru'ya a perception beyond knowledge, or a specific knowledge? Find these two positions.
3
Understand the conditions for ru'ya
Those who consider ru'ya a distinct perception differ on conditions like the eye's ray, extreme distance, etc. Note the Mu'tazili requirement of these conditions.
4
See the positions of Mu'tazila and Ash'ari
The Mu'tazila deny ru'ya as impossible; al-Ash'ari affirms it as possible and obligatory by revelation. The passage also mentions two ways to understand ru'ya as a specific knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نهاية الإقدام في علم الكلام (٦) القاعدة السادسة عشرة في جواز رؤية الباري (١) تعالى عقلا ووجوبها (٢) سمعا. لم يصر صائر من أهل القبلة إلى تجويز اتصال أشعة من البصر بذاته تعالى، أو انطباع شبح يتمثل في الحاسة منه، وانفصال شيء من الرائي والمرئي واتصاله بهما، لكن أهل الأصول اختلفوا في أن الرؤية إدراك وراء العلم أم علم مخصوص، ومن زعم أنه إدراك وراء العلم اختلف في اشتراط البنية واتصال الشعاع، ونفي القرب المفرط والبعد المفرط وتوسط الهوى المشف، فشرطها المعتزلة ونفوا رؤية الباري تعالى بالأبصار (۳) نفي الاستحالة، والأشعري أثبتها إثبات الجواز على الإطلاق والوجوب بحكم الوعد، ثم ردد قوله: إنه علم مخصوص؛ أي لا يتعلق إلا بالموجود، أم هو إدراك حكمه حكم العلم في التعلق؛ أي: لا يتأثر من المرئي ولا يؤثر فيه، ونحن نورد كلام الفريقين على الرسم المعهود. قالت الأشعرية: الموجودات اشتركت في قضايا واختلفت في قضايا، والرؤية قد تعلقت بالمختلفات منها والمتفقات ولا يجوز أن يكون المصحح للرؤية ما يختلف فيه فإنه يوجب أن يكون لحكم واحد علتان مختلفتان، وهذا غير جائز في المعقولات أو يلزم أن يكون لحكم عام علة خاصة هي أخص من معلولها، وما يتفق فيه الجوهر والعرض إما الوجود أو الحدوث والحدوث لا يجوز أن يكون مصححا للرؤية، فإن الحدوث هو وجود مسبوق بعدم، والعدم لا تأثير له في الحكم، فبقي الوجود مصححا بالضرورة، وهذا تقسيم حاصر، فإن الرؤية بالاتفاق تعلقت بالجوهر والعرض وهما قد اختلفا من كل وجه سوى الوجود والحدوث، وقد بطل الحدوث فتعين الوجود، ولا يلزم على هذه الطريقة انتشار الأقسام، كما لزم على طريق الأصحاب غير استبعاد محض المعتزلة في قولهم: لو كان كل موجود مرئيا لكان العلم والقدرة والطعم والرائحة وما سوى اللون والمتلون مرئيا، ولكان نفس الرؤية مرئية بالرؤية وهذا محال. ويلتزم أبو الحسن جواز الرؤية في جميع الموجودات على الإطلاق، خصوصاً إذا قال: الرؤية علم مخصوص فالعلم كما يتعلق بهذه الموجودات كذلك الرؤية ولا فرق إلا أن العلم المطلق يتعلق بالواجب والجائز والمستحيل وهذا العلم لا يتعلق إلا بالموجود فقط، فإنه يقتضي تعيين المرئي والتعيين يتحقق في العدم، وإن سلكنا طريقة العلم فهو أسهل، فإن العلم من حيث هو علم نوع واحد وحقيقة واحدة، وإذا جوز تعلق العلم به فقد جوز تعلق الرؤية به
The End of the Advance in the Science of Kalam (6) The Sixteenth Principle: On the permissibility of seeing the Creator (1), exalted, intellectually and its obligation (2) by revelation. No one among the people of the qibla has gone to the permissibility of the connection of rays from the eye to His essence, exalted, or the impression of an image that takes shape in the sense from Him, or the separation of something from the seer and the seen and its connection to them. But the people of principles disagreed on whether seeing (ru'ya) is a perception beyond knowledge (ilm) or a specific knowledge. And whoever claimed that it is a perception beyond knowledge disagreed on the condition of the organ and the connection of the ray, and the negation of excessive nearness and excessive distance and the mediation of transparent air. So the Mu'tazila conditioned it and negated seeing the Creator, exalted, with sight (absar) as a negation of impossibility, and al-Ash'ari affirmed it as an affirmation of absolute permissibility and obligation by virtue of the promise. Then he repeated his saying: it is a specific knowledge, i.e., it relates only to the existent, or is it a perception whose rule is the rule of knowledge in relation, i.e., it is not affected by the seen nor does it affect it. And we present the speech of the two groups in the customary manner. The Ash'ariyya said: Existents share in some matters and differ in some matters, and seeing (ru'ya) has related to both the differing and the agreeing aspects. It is not permissible that the ground (musahhih) for seeing be that in which they differ, for that would necessitate that one judgment have two different causes, and this is not permissible in intelligibles, or it would necessitate that a general judgment have a specific cause that is more specific than its effect. And that in which substance (jawhar) and accident (arad) agree is either existence (wujud) or occurrence (huduth). Occurrence cannot be the ground for seeing, for occurrence is existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence has no effect in judgment. So existence remains as the ground necessarily. And this is an exhaustive division, for seeing, by agreement, has related to substance and accident, and the two have differed in every respect except existence and occurrence. Occurrence has been invalidated, so existence is determined. And this method does not necessitate the spread of categories, as happened in the method of the companions, [it is] not mere rejection of the Mu'tazila in their saying: If every existent were visible, then knowledge, power, taste, smell, and what is other than color and the colored would be visible, and vision itself would be visible by vision, and this is impossible. And Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) commits to the permissibility of seeing all existents absolutely, especially when he says: Seeing is specific knowledge. For knowledge, just as it relates to these existents, so too does seeing, and there is no difference except that absolute knowledge relates to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, while this knowledge relates only to the existent alone, for it necessitates specification of the seen, and specification is realized in non-existence. And if we follow the method of knowledge, it is easier, for knowledge qua knowledge is one type and one reality. And if the relation of knowledge to it is allowed, then the relation of seeing to it is allowed.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the Ash'ari proof for the intellectual permissibility of seeing God (ru'ya), along with an objection from the Mu'tazila and a response. The author begins by stating the title: the sixteenth principle concerning the permissibility of seeing God intellectually and its obligation by revelation. He then notes that no one from the Ahl al-Qibla holds the view that physical rays connect to God's essence or that an image impresses upon the sense, as those are impossible for God. However, the 'people of principles' (theologians) disagreed on whether seeing (ru'ya) is a perception distinct from knowledge (ilm) or a specific kind of knowledge. Those who say it is a distinct perception further disagreed on conditions like the physical organ, connection of rays, absence of extreme distance/proximity, and transparency of medium. The Mu'tazila conditioned these and denied the possibility of seeing God, while al-Ash'ari affirmed both its logical possibility and its necessity based on the divine promise. Al-Ash'ari then considered two possibilities: that seeing is a specific knowledge relating only to existents, or a perception with the same relational rule as knowledge (i.e., not affected by the object and not affecting it). The author then presents the Ash'ari argument. The Ash'ariyya argue that existents share some qualities and differ in others. Since seeing can relate to both shared and differing aspects, the ground for visibility cannot be in what they differ, because that would require different causes for one judgment. Nor can it be a general cause that is more specific than its effect. Substance and accident agree only in existence (wujud) or occurrence (huduth). Occurrence is existence preceded by non-existence; non-existence has no effect, so occurrence cannot be the ground. Thus existence must be the ground for visibility. This is an exhaustive division: all existents are either substance or accident, and they agree only in existence and occurrence. Occurrence is invalidated, so existence is the ground. This method avoids spreading categories, unlike the Mu'tazila's claim that if every existent were visible, then non-physical entities like knowledge, power, etc. would be visible, and vision itself would be visible, which they deem impossible. Al-Ash'ari himself accepts the permissibility of seeing all existents, especially if seeing is defined as specific knowledge, because knowledge relates to all existents, and seeing, being knowledge, would relate to them too, except that absolute knowledge relates to the necessary, possible, and impossible, while specific knowledge relates only to existents. If one follows the knowledge analogy, it is easier: knowledge is one reality, so if it can relate to something, seeing can too.

Key Terms
رؤية
علم
جواز
وجوب

According to the passage, what do the Mu'tazila and Ash'ari disagree on regarding ru'ya?
A) Whether ru'ya requires physical conditions
B) Whether ru'ya is a perception beyond knowledge or a specific knowledge
C) Whether ru'ya is possible for God
D) Whether ru'ya is an image impression

Key Takeaway
The foundational debate on ru'ya is about its nature—whether it is a perception beyond knowledge or a specific knowledge—and this shapes whether it can apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is not seen by physical eyes, what does it mean for you to 'see' Him through knowledge or faith? How can you increase that perception in your daily life?
?
The Mu'tazila denied ru'ya because they applied human sensory conditions to God. Are there any ways you inadvertently limit your understanding of God by projecting human limitations onto Him? What step can you take today to think of God with more reverence?
?
Al-Ash'ari affirmed ru'ya as possible by revelation. Do you trust God's promise of seeing Him in the hereafter more than your own reasoning? What would change in your heart if you truly yearned for that vision?

The Ash'ari Proof from Existence
Presents the Ash'ari proof that existence is the ground for visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that existence (wujud) is the rational ground for visibility.

Core Idea
Existence alone makes something visible; no other property is needed.

The Ash'ari argument proceeds by examining what substances and accidents share. Since seeing applies to both, the ground must be something common. This lesson walks through the logic that eliminates occurrence (huduth) and leaves existence as the only possible ground.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari proof
Read the passage carefully. It starts with the premise that existents share some qualities and differ in others. Seeing relates to both the shared and differing aspects.
2
Understand the elimination of differing qualities
The ground for visibility cannot be in what differs, because that would require different causes for the same judgment or a cause more specific than its effect.
3
Identify the two common qualities
Substance and accident agree only in existence (wujud) and occurrence (huduth). Occurrence is existence preceded by non-existence; non-existence has no effect, so it is invalid.
4
Conclude that existence is the ground
By process of elimination, existence remains as the necessary ground for visibility.

Source Text

No one among the people of the qibla has gone to the permissibility of the connection of rays from the eye to His essence, exalted, or the impression of an image that takes shape in the sense from Him, or the separation of something from the seer and the seen and its connection to them. And we present the speech of the two groups in the customary manner. The Ash'ariyya said: Existents share in some matters and differ in some matters, and seeing (ru'ya) has related to both the differing and the agreeing aspects. It is not permissible that the ground (musahhih) for seeing be that in which they differ, for that would necessitate that one judgment have two different causes, and this is not permissible in intelligibles, or it would necessitate that a general judgment have a specific cause that is more specific than its effect. Occurrence cannot be the ground for seeing, for occurrence is existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence has no effect in judgment. So existence remains as the ground necessarily. And this is an exhaustive division, for seeing, by agreement, has related to substance and accident, and the two have differed in every respect except existence and occurrence. And this method does not necessitate the spread of categories, as happened in the method of the companions, [it is] not mere rejection of the Mu'tazila in their saying: If every existent were visible, then knowledge, power, taste, smell, and what is other than color and the colored would be visible, and vision itself would be visible by vision, and this is impossible. Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the Ash'ari proof for the intellectual permissibility of seeing God (ru'ya), along with an objection from the Mu'tazila and a response. The author begins by stating the title: the sixteenth principle concerning the permissibility of seeing God intellectually and its obligation by revelation. The Mu'tazila conditioned these and denied the possibility of seeing God, while al-Ash'ari affirmed both its logical possibility and its necessity based on the divine promise. Al-Ash'ari then considered two possibilities: that seeing is a specific knowledge relating only to existents, or a perception with the same relational rule as knowledge (i.e., not affected by the object and not affecting it). The author then presents the Ash'ari argument. The Ash'ariyya argue that existents share some qualities and differ in others. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that only material objects are visible, or that existence is not enough.
Correction: The Ash'ari proof argues that existence itself is the sufficient ground; no additional materiality is needed.

Why does the Ash'ari proof eliminate occurrence (huduth) as the ground for visibility?
A) Because occurrence is not shared by all existents
B) Because occurrence involves non-existence, which has no effect

D) Because occurrence is not a real property

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof establishes that existence (wujud) is the sole rational ground for visibility, making it possible for God to be seen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence alone suffices for God to be seen, what does that mean for your certainty in His existence? Are you living as if His existence is enough to demand your worship?
?
The Ash'ari argument removes all conditions for visibility except existence. What condition do you unknowingly place on your relationship with God that has no basis in revelation or reason?
?
If non-existence has no effect in judgment, what does that imply about your fear of losing worldly existence? Does your hope in God's visibility outweigh your attachment to temporary existence?

The Mu'tazili Objection and Ash'ari Response
Presents the Mu'tazili objection and al-Ash'ari's response using the knowledge analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili objection that if every existent is visible, then non-physical entities would be visible, and the Ash'ari response that seeing is a specific knowledge.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili objection is answered by defining ru'ya as a specific knowledge that only relates to existents.

After presenting the proof, the author addresses the Mu'tazili objection: if existence makes everything visible, then abstract entities like knowledge and power would be visible, and even vision itself—an absurd consequence. Al-Ash'ari responds by committing to the permissibility of seeing all existents, and by equating ru'ya with a specific knowledge that relates only to existents, analogous to general knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili objection
The objection claims that if every existent were visible, then non-sensible entities like knowledge and power would be visible, and vision itself would be visible by vision—an impossibility.
2
Understand al-Ash'ari's commitment
Al-Ash'ari accepts the permissibility of seeing all existents absolutely, especially if ru'ya is considered specific knowledge.
3
Follow the knowledge analogy
If ru'ya is a specific knowledge, then just as general knowledge relates to all existents, so does ru'ya, except that general knowledge can relate to non-existents while ru'ya only to existents.
4
Conclude the response
The passage ends by stating that if knowledge can relate to something, then ru'ya as specific knowledge can too, since both share the same reality as knowledge.

نهاية الإقدام في علم الكلام (٦) القاعدة السادسة عشرة في جواز رؤية الباري (١) تعالى عقلا ووجوبها (٢) سمعا. لم يصر صائر من أهل القبلة إلى تجويز اتصال أشعة من البصر بذاته تعالى، أو انطباع شبح يتمثل في الحاسة منه، وانفصال شيء من الرائي والمرئي واتصاله بهما، لكن أهل الأصول اختلفوا في أن الرؤية إدراك وراء العلم أم علم مخصوص، ومن زعم أنه إدراك وراء العلم اختلف في اشتراط البنية واتصال الشعاع، ونفي القرب المفرط والبعد المفرط وتوسط الهوى المشف، فشرطها المعتزلة ونفوا رؤية الباري تعالى بالأبصار (۳) نفي الاستحالة، والأشعري أثبتها إثبات الجواز على الإطلاق والوجوب بحكم الوعد، ثم ردد قوله: إنه علم مخصوص؛ أي لا يتعلق إلا بالموجود، أم هو إدراك حكمه حكم العلم في التعلق؛ أي: لا يتأثر من المرئي ولا يؤثر فيه، ونحن نورد كلام الفريقين على الرسم المعهود. قالت الأشعرية: الموجودات اشتركت في قضايا واختلفت في قضايا، والرؤية قد تعلقت بالمختلفات منها والمتفقات ولا يجوز أن يكون المصحح للرؤية ما يختلف فيه فإنه يوجب أن يكون لحكم واحد علتان مختلفتان، وهذا غير جائز في المعقولات أو يلزم أن يكون لحكم عام علة خاصة هي أخص من معلولها، وما يتفق فيه الجوهر والعرض إما الوجود أو الحدوث والحدوث لا يجوز أن يكون مصححا للرؤية، فإن الحدوث هو وجود مسبوق بعدم، والعدم لا تأثير له في الحكم، فبقي الوجود مصححا بالضرورة، وهذا تقسيم حاصر، فإن الرؤية بالاتفاق تعلقت بالجوهر والعرض وهما قد اختلفا من كل وجه سوى الوجود والحدوث، وقد بطل الحدوث فتعين الوجود، ولا يلزم على هذه الطريقة انتشار الأقسام، كما لزم على طريق الأصحاب غير استبعاد محض المعتزلة في قولهم: لو كان كل موجود مرئيا لكان العلم والقدرة والطعم والرائحة وما سوى اللون والمتلون مرئيا، ولكان نفس الرؤية مرئية بالرؤية وهذا محال. ويلتزم أبو الحسن جواز الرؤية في جميع الموجودات على الإطلاق، خصوصاً إذا قال: الرؤية علم مخصوص فالعلم كما يتعلق بهذه الموجودات كذلك الرؤية ولا فرق إلا أن العلم المطلق يتعلق بالواجب والجائز والمستحيل وهذا العلم لا يتعلق إلا بالموجود فقط، فإنه يقتضي تعيين المرئي والتعيين يتحقق في العدم، وإن سلكنا طريقة العلم فهو أسهل، فإن العلم من حيث هو علم نوع واحد وحقيقة واحدة، وإذا جوز تعلق العلم به فقد جوز تعلق الرؤية به
The End of the Advance in the Science of Kalam (6) The Sixteenth Principle: On the permissibility of seeing the Creator (1), exalted, intellectually and its obligation (2) by revelation. No one among the people of the qibla has gone to the permissibility of the connection of rays from the eye to His essence, exalted, or the impression of an image that takes shape in the sense from Him, or the separation of something from the seer and the seen and its connection to them. But the people of principles disagreed on whether seeing (ru'ya) is a perception beyond knowledge (ilm) or a specific knowledge. And whoever claimed that it is a perception beyond knowledge disagreed on the condition of the organ and the connection of the ray, and the negation of excessive nearness and excessive distance and the mediation of transparent air. So the Mu'tazila conditioned it and negated seeing the Creator, exalted, with sight (absar) as a negation of impossibility, and al-Ash'ari affirmed it as an affirmation of absolute permissibility and obligation by virtue of the promise. Then he repeated his saying: it is a specific knowledge, i.e., it relates only to the existent, or is it a perception whose rule is the rule of knowledge in relation, i.e., it is not affected by the seen nor does it affect it. And we present the speech of the two groups in the customary manner. The Ash'ariyya said: Existents share in some matters and differ in some matters, and seeing (ru'ya) has related to both the differing and the agreeing aspects. It is not permissible that the ground (musahhih) for seeing be that in which they differ, for that would necessitate that one judgment have two different causes, and this is not permissible in intelligibles, or it would necessitate that a general judgment have a specific cause that is more specific than its effect. And that in which substance (jawhar) and accident (arad) agree is either existence (wujud) or occurrence (huduth). Occurrence cannot be the ground for seeing, for occurrence is existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence has no effect in judgment. So existence remains as the ground necessarily. And this is an exhaustive division, for seeing, by agreement, has related to substance and accident, and the two have differed in every respect except existence and occurrence. Occurrence has been invalidated, so existence is determined. And this method does not necessitate the spread of categories, as happened in the method of the companions, [it is] not mere rejection of the Mu'tazila in their saying: If every existent were visible, then knowledge, power, taste, smell, and what is other than color and the colored would be visible, and vision itself would be visible by vision, and this is impossible. And Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) commits to the permissibility of seeing all existents absolutely, especially when he says: Seeing is specific knowledge. For knowledge, just as it relates to these existents, so too does seeing, and there is no difference except that absolute knowledge relates to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, while this knowledge relates only to the existent alone, for it necessitates specification of the seen, and specification is realized in non-existence. And if we follow the method of knowledge, it is easier, for knowledge qua knowledge is one type and one reality. And if the relation of knowledge to it is allowed, then the relation of seeing to it is allowed.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the Ash'ari proof for the intellectual permissibility of seeing God (ru'ya), along with an objection from the Mu'tazila and a response. The author begins by stating the title: the sixteenth principle concerning the permissibility of seeing God intellectually and its obligation by revelation. He then notes that no one from the Ahl al-Qibla holds the view that physical rays connect to God's essence or that an image impresses upon the sense, as those are impossible for God. However, the 'people of principles' (theologians) disagreed on whether seeing (ru'ya) is a perception distinct from knowledge (ilm) or a specific kind of knowledge. Those who say it is a distinct perception further disagreed on conditions like the physical organ, connection of rays, absence of extreme distance/proximity, and transparency of medium. The Mu'tazila conditioned these and denied the possibility of seeing God, while al-Ash'ari affirmed both its logical possibility and its necessity based on the divine promise. Al-Ash'ari then considered two possibilities: that seeing is a specific knowledge relating only to existents, or a perception with the same relational rule as knowledge (i.e., not affected by the object and not affecting it). The author then presents the Ash'ari argument. The Ash'ariyya argue that existents share some qualities and differ in others. Since seeing can relate to both shared and differing aspects, the ground for visibility cannot be in what they differ, because that would require different causes for one judgment. Nor can it be a general cause that is more specific than its effect. Substance and accident agree only in existence (wujud) or occurrence (huduth). Occurrence is existence preceded by non-existence; non-existence has no effect, so occurrence cannot be the ground. Thus existence must be the ground for visibility. This is an exhaustive division: all existents are either substance or accident, and they agree only in existence and occurrence. Occurrence is invalidated, so existence is the ground. This method avoids spreading categories, unlike the Mu'tazila's claim that if every existent were visible, then non-physical entities like knowledge, power, etc. would be visible, and vision itself would be visible, which they deem impossible. Al-Ash'ari himself accepts the permissibility of seeing all existents, especially if seeing is defined as specific knowledge, because knowledge relates to all existents, and seeing, being knowledge, would relate to them too, except that absolute knowledge relates to the necessary, possible, and impossible, while specific knowledge relates only to existents. If one follows the knowledge analogy, it is easier: knowledge is one reality, so if it can relate to something, seeing can too.

Key Terms
رؤية
علم
موجود
مستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Ru'ya is a physical process, knowledge is not

D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazili objection is to define ru'ya as a specific knowledge that only applies to existents, thus avoiding the absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson defines ru'ya as a specific knowledge. Have you ever assumed that knowing God is limited to intellectual concepts, while shying away from the reality that God desires to be seen? What is preventing you from longing for the vision of Allah in the afterlife as a real, direct perception—not just an idea?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that attributing vision to non-physical entities leads to absurdity. Are there areas in your own belief where you limit Allah's ability or attributes based on your own logical frameworks? Will you submit to what Allah has promised, even if it challenges your sense of what is 'possible'?
?
If ru'ya is a specific knowledge that relates only to existents, then your certainty in Allah's existence should translate into a profound desire to see Him. On a scale of 1 to 10, how urgent is that desire in your daily life? What would it take to move it higher?

Existence as the Basis for Seeing God
The author argues that existence, not temporality, is the basis for the possibility of seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence rather than temporality is the rational basis for the possibility of seeing God, and how this argument counters the Mu'tazila denial.

Core Idea
Existence, not temporality, makes something visible.

This passage presents a key argument in the debate about seeing God. The author, a Sunni theologian, proves that the basis for visibility is existence itself. By examining what substances and accidents share, he eliminates temporality and establishes existence as the necessary ground for vision, thereby defending the possibility of seeing God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common factor
Recognize that both substances and accidents exist and are temporal, so only existence or temporality can be the basis for vision. Note that vision applies to both.
This sets up the exhaustive division.
2
Eliminate temporality
Understand why temporality cannot be the basis: it is existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence has no effect on a positive judgment like visibility.
The argument hinges on non-existence being causally inert.
3
Confirm existence as the basis
See that through the two methods (general judgment and exhaustive division), existence remains as the only valid basis. Then note the Mu'tazila objection and why the author dismisses it as absurd.
The objection will be addressed later in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعقولات أو يلزم أن يكون لحكم عام علة خاصة هي أخص من معلولها، وما يتفق فيه الجوهر والعرض إما الوجود أو الحدوث، والحدوث لا يجوز أن يكون مصححا للرؤية، فإن الحدوث هو وجود مسبوق بعدم، والعدم لا تأثير له في الحكم، فبقي الوجود مصححا بالضرورة أو هذا تقسيم حاصرة، فإن الرؤية بالاتفاق تعلقت بالجوهر والعرض وهما قد اختلفا من كل وجه سوى الوجود والحدوث، وقد بطل الحدوث فتعين الوجود، ولا يلزم على هذه الطريقة انتشار الأقسام، كما لزم على طريق الأصحاب غير استبعاد محض المعتزلة في قولهم: لو كان كل موجود مرئيا لكان العلم والقدرة والطعم والرائحة وما سوى اللون والمتلون مرئيا، ولكان نفس الرؤية مرئية بالرؤية وهذا محال.
The intelligibles or it must be that a general judgment has a specific cause that is more specific than its effect, and that in which substance and accident agree is either existence or temporality, and temporality cannot be a correct basis (musahhih) for vision, for temporality is existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence has no effect on the judgment, so existence remains as the basis by necessity. Or this is an all-encompassing division: for vision by agreement attaches to substance and accident, and they have differed in every respect except existence and temporality, and temporality has been invalidated, so existence is determined. And no division of categories arises from this method, as arose from the method of the companions, except the mere absurdity of the Mu'tazila in their saying: If every existent were visible, then knowledge, power, taste, smell, and everything other than color and colored would be visible, and vision itself would be visible by vision, and this is impossible.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for establishing the basis (musahhih) for the possibility of vision (ru'ya) of God. The author is responding to the Mu'tazila who deny that God can be seen. The argument proceeds by looking for a common factor between substances and accidents, since both types of entities are seen by agreement. The possible common factors are existence (wujud) and temporality (huduth). Temporality cannot be the basis because it is existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence has no effect; thus the basis must be existence. Then the author gives an alternative formulation: vision attaches to substance and accident, which differ in all respects except existence and temporality; since temporality is invalid, existence is confirmed. This method avoids the problem of category proliferation that occurs in the method of the earlier scholars. The only objection is the mere absurdity of the Mu'tazila that if all existents were visible, then abstract accidents like knowledge would be visible, and vision itself would be visible. This is dismissed as impossible, but will be addressed later.

Key Terms
مصحح (musahhih)
الوجود (wujud)
الحدوث (huduth)
الرؤية (ru'ya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporality (huduth) could be the basis for visibility because everything that is temporal is visible.
Correction: Temporality involves non-existence, which has no effect on a positive judgment like visibility; therefore it cannot be the basis.

Checkpoint

B) Existence (wujud)
C) Color (lawn)
D) Substance (jawhar)

Key Takeaway
Existence, not temporality, is the rational basis for the possibility of seeing God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence alone makes something visible, what does this imply about your certainty of seeing Allah in the afterlife? Are you living with that hope as a concrete conviction, or just a vague possibility? Commit today to affirming this belief with your heart and actions.
?
The Mu'tazila limited visibility to physical objects because they could not conceive of seeing a non-physical being. Where in your own life do you limit God by your rational categories—perhaps in your expectations of His mercy, power, or how He answers prayers? Will you repent of confining Him to your mental boxes?
?
This proof uses logic to establish a cornerstone of your faith, yet many Muslims rely solely on tradition. Have you ever personally engaged with rational proofs for your beliefs? Will you dedicate time to study the creed (aqidah) with evidence so that your faith is built on certainty, not blind imitation?

Ash'ari on the Visibility of All Existents
Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari argues for the visibility of all existents by comparing vision to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari argues that all existent things are potentially visible by comparing vision to knowledge.

Core Idea
Vision is a specific knowledge, so it can attach to all existents.

Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari responds to the Mu'tazila objection that not all existents can be visible. He defines vision as a specific type of knowledge, making the visibility of all existents consistent with how knowledge relates to things. This passage explains his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the key terms: 'ru'ya' and 'ilm makhsus'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the core claim
Find where Abu al-Hasan states that vision is a specific knowledge. Understand that this is the foundation of his argument.
3
Compare vision and knowledge
Notice how he compares vision to knowledge: both attach to existents, but knowledge also attaches to non-existents. Vision requires specification.
Think about why specification matters for vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلتزم أبو الحسن جواز الرؤية في جميع الموجودات على الإطلاق، خصوصاً إذا قال: الرؤية علم مخصوص، فالعلم كما يتعلق بهذه الموجودات كذلك الرؤية ولا فرق إلا أن العلم المطلق يتعلق بالواجب والجائز والمستحيل وهذا العلم لا يتعلق إلا بالموجود فقط، فإنه يقتضي تعيين المرئي والتعيين يتحقق في العدم، وإن سلكنا طريقة العلم فهو أسهل، فإن العلم من حيث هو علم نوع واحد وحقيقة واحدة، وإذا جوز تعلق العلم به فقد جوز تعلق الرؤية به.
And Abu al-Hasan commits himself to the permissibility of vision in all existent things absolutely, especially when he says: vision is a specific knowledge (ilm makhsus). For just as knowledge attaches to these existent things, so does vision, and there is no difference except that absolute knowledge attaches to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, while this knowledge attaches only to the existent alone, for it requires the specification of the visible, and specification is realized in non-existence. And if we follow the method of knowledge, it is easier, for knowledge, inasmuch as it is knowledge, is one type and one reality, and if the attachment of knowledge to it is permissible, then the attachment of vision to it is also permissible.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents the position of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari in response to the Mu'tazila objection that if all existents were visible, then abstract accidents like knowledge and power would be visible, which is absurd. Abu al-Hasan accepts the consequence: all existents are potentially visible. He explains that vision is a specific type of knowledge (ilm makhsus). Just as knowledge relates to all existent things, so does vision. The only difference is that absolute knowledge can relate to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, whereas this specific knowledge (vision) relates only to the existent, because vision requires specifying the visible, and specification can be realized even in non-existence? The text is a bit ambiguous. Then the author says that if we follow the method of knowledge, it is easier: knowledge is one type and one reality, so if knowledge can attach to a thing, vision can too.

Key Terms

الرؤية
علم مخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that accepting the visibility of all existents is absurd because it would mean abstract things like knowledge are visible.
Correction: Ash'ari sees it as consistent: vision is a type of knowledge, so if knowledge can relate to abstract things, vision can too, but only to existents.

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari defends the visibility of all existents by considering vision a specific knowledge, thus extending its attachment to all existent things.

Al-Isfara'ini's Argument for Vision of God
Al-Isfara'ini argues that vision is a meaning like knowledge, neither affecting nor affected.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Isfara'ini defends the possibility of seeing God by redefining vision as a meaning that neither affects nor is affected.

Core Idea
Vision is like knowledge: it can attach to God without affecting or being affected.

Al-Isfara'ini presents a nuanced argument for the possibility of seeing God. He redefines vision as a 'meaning' (ma'na) in the eye that does not influence or be influenced by the seen object. This clever move sidesteps the impossibility that would arise if vision involved physical interaction. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Problem
The theological controversy: Can God be seen? Some argue it's impossible because vision requires physical interaction. Al-Isfara'ini aims to prove it's rationally possible.
2
Grasp al-Isfara'ini's Definition
He defines vision as a 'meaning' (ma'na) that does not affect the visible object and is not affected by it. This is the key innovation.
Note the contrast with other senses like touch, which involve causation.
3
Contrast with Other Senses
Other senses involve influence (e.g., hearing involves sound waves affecting the ear). Vision, according to this definition, is free from such interaction.
Al-Isfara'ini argues that impossibility only follows if vision affected or was affected.
4
Connect to Knowledge
Since vision is like knowledge (which can be attributed to God without issue), and it's agreed that knowledge attaches to God, then vision can too. This completes the rational proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد سلك الأستاذ أبو إسحاق طريقة قريبة من هذه فقال: الرؤية معنى لا تؤثر في المرئي ولا تتأثر منه، فإن حكمه حكم العلم بخلاف سائر الحواس فإنها تؤثر وتتأثر، وإنما يلزم الاستحالة فيه أن لو تأثرت الرؤية من المرئي أو تأثر المرئي من الرؤية، وكل ما هذا سبيله فهو جائز التعلق بالقديم والحادث، وكل مؤثر ومتأثر فهو مستحيل عندنا كما هو مستحيل عندكم، ولا كلفة في هذه الطريقة إلا إثبات معنى في البصر لا يؤثر ولا يتأثر، وقد أثبتنا من قبل أن الإدراك البصري لا يستدعي اتصال شعاع بالمرئي ولا انفصال شيء من الرائي، فبطل الوجهان انتفى التأثير والتأثر وصار المعنى كالعلم أو هو من جنس العلم، وقد تقرر الاتفاق على جواز تعلق العلم به وهذا نهاية ما قيل في إثبات الجواز من جهة العقل.
And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq pursued a method close to this, and said: Vision is a meaning (ma'na) that does not affect the visible and is not affected by it, for its judgment is the judgment of knowledge, unlike the other senses, for they affect and are affected. Impossibility would only follow if the vision were affected by the visible or the visible were affected by the vision, and everything that is of this way is permissible to attach to both the eternal and the originated, and every affecter and affected is impossible according to us as it is impossible according to you. There is no difficulty in this method except proving a meaning in the eye that neither affects nor is affected, and we have already established that visual perception (idrak basari) does not require the connection of a ray to the visible nor the detachment of anything from the seer; so both aspects are invalidated, and influence and being influenced are negated, and the meaning becomes like knowledge or is of the genus of knowledge, and it has been established by agreement that the attachment of knowledge to it is permissible, and this is the ultimate of what has been said in establishing the permissibility from the standpoint of reason.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents al-Isfara'ini's approach to proving the possibility of seeing God. He defines vision (ru'ya) as a meaning (ma'na) that neither affects the seen object nor is affected by it. This is similar to knowledge, making it possible to attach to both the eternal and the originated. In contrast, other senses involve influence, e.g., touching causes heat. Impossibility would only arise if the vision affected or was affected. The only difficulty is proving the existence of such a meaning in the eye. Al-Isfara'ini claims this has been established because visual perception does not require a ray from the eye to the object or anything detaching from the seer; thus there is no influence. Therefore, vision is like knowledge. Since it is agreed that knowledge can attach to God, vision can too. This is the rational proof for permissibility.

Key Terms
أبو إسحاق (Abu Ishaq)
معنى (ma'na)
الإدراك البصري (idrak basari)
التأثير (ta'thir)
التأثر (ta'aththur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think vision necessarily involves being affected by light or rays, making physical interaction essential.
Correction: Al-Isfara'ini argues that vision is a meaning that does not require physical interaction; it is like knowledge, which can be attributed to God without any physical process.

Checkpoint
According to al-Isfara'ini, what distinguishes vision from other senses?
A) Vision involves a ray from the eye
B) Vision neither affects nor is affected
C) Vision requires direct contact
D) Vision is a bodily process

Key Takeaway
Al-Isfara'ini's key move is to define vision as a non-interactive meaning, making it analogous to knowledge, which is uncontroversially attributable to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Isfara'ini equates vision with knowledge in permissibility. But is your knowledge of God today a living reality that stirs your soul, or merely an abstract fact? Repent from the indifference that treats His presence as a theory.
?
This argument makes God's visibility rationally possible. Yet are you living as if you will actually see your Lord, preparing your heart for that moment, or are you content with distant belief? Pray for the sincerity to long for His face.

The Mu'tazila Objection: Vision's Permissibility Has No Cause
Mu'tazila argument against a cause for vision's permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that the permission to see Allah cannot have a causal basis, as it belongs to judgments that are not causally explicable.

Core Idea
Some judgments have no cause; vision's permission is among them.

The Mu'tazila challenge the Ash'ari search for a cause (musahhih) for the possibility of seeing Allah. They argue that some judgments, like spatial occupancy and essential attributes, are inherently uncaused. Vision's permissibility falls in this category. They support this with an analogy from knowledge, which attaches to all things without a common basis.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that the passage is incomplete—it cuts off mid-sentence. Focus on the core argument presented.
The response to this objection is not provided in this source chunk.
2
Identify the two categories of judgments
The Mu'tazila divide judgments into those that have a cause (like being knowing) and those that do not (like occupying space). Find these examples in the text.
3
Analyze the analogy with knowledge
The Mu'tazila argue that knowledge attaches to the necessary, possible, and impossible without needing a common cause. This shows that some things can exist without a basis. Ask yourself: How does this analogy apply to vision?
The analogy challenges the need for a 'correct basis' for vision.
4
Consider the implications
If vision is like knowledge (a specific knowledge or meaning), then it too should not require a cause. Reflect on how this objection undermines the Ash'ari position.
The passage ends before the Ash'ari response is given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قالت المعتزلة: قد طلبتم للرؤية علة مصححة وتعين لكم الوجود، فما أنكرتم أن جواز الرؤية من الأحكام التي لا تعلل، فإن من الأحكام ما يعلل كالعالمية والقادرية، ومنها ما لا يعلل كالتحيز وقبول العرض وجميع الصفات النفسية، أليس تعلق العلم بالمعلومات لا يستدعي مصححا إذ تعلق بالواجب والجائز والمستحيل، ولم توجد قضية هي أعم من الأقسام الثلاثة حتى تكون تلك القضية هي المصححة لتعلق العلم بها وكونها معلومة هي نفس تعلق العلم بها فتكون العلة والمعلول واحدا، وإذا قررتم أن الرؤية إما علم مخصوص أو معنى في حكم العلم، ثم لم
And when the Mu'tazila say: You have sought for vision a correct basis (illa musahhiha) and existence has been determined for you, so what prevents you from admitting that the permissibility of vision is among the judgments that are not causally explicable (la tu'allal)? For among judgments are those that are causally explicable, like being knowing and being powerful, and among them are those that are not causally explicable, like occupying space (tahayyuz) and receiving accidents (qubul al-'arad) and all essential attributes (sifat nafsiyya). Does not the attachment of knowledge to known things not require a correct basis, since it attaches to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, and there is no proposition that is more general than the three divisions so that that proposition would be the correct basis for the attachment of knowledge to them? And the fact that they are known is itself the attachment of knowledge to them, so the cause and effect would be one. And if you have established that vision is either a specific knowledge or a meaning in the judgment of knowledge, then [you have not]...
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila objection to the Ash'ari argument that existence is the basis (musahhih) for the possibility of vision. The Mu'tazila argue that the permissibility of vision (jawaz al-ru'ya) is among the judgments that cannot be causally explained (la tu'allal). They give examples: some judgments are explicable (like being knowing or powerful), but others are not, such as occupying space (tahayyuz), receiving accidents (qubul al-'arad), and all essential attributes (sifat nafsiyya). They then draw an analogy with knowledge: the attachment of knowledge to its objects does not require a basis, because knowledge attaches to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, and there is no common factor among these three categories that could serve as the basis. In fact, the knownness of something is the same as knowledge's attachment to it, making cause and effect identical. So if the Ash'arites say vision is a specific knowledge or a meaning like knowledge, then it should also need no basis. The passage ends abruptly with 'thum lam' (then not...), indicating the response may be cut off or continues.

Abu Ishaq's Argument for the Permissibility of Seeing God
Abu Ishaq argues that vision is a meaning like knowledge, permissible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Ishaq's rational proof that vision of God is possible because vision, like knowledge, does not involve physical effect or being affected.

Core Idea
Vision is permissible for God because it is a meaning like knowledge, not a physical interaction.

Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini presents a concise rational argument for the permissibility of seeing God (ru'ya). He draws an analogy between vision and knowledge, arguing that just as knowledge can relate to God without affecting Him, so can vision. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the analogy with knowledge.
2
Identify the key analogy
Abu Ishaq compares vision to knowledge, stating that both are meanings that do not affect nor are affected. Contrast this with other senses like touch.
3
Understand the condition for impossibility
The argument states that impossibility would only follow if vision involved effect or being affected. Since it does not, vision is permissible.
4
Connect to previous reasoning
The passage references that visual perception does not require a ray or physical connection, which supports the claim that vision is like knowledge.
This builds on earlier discussions about the nature of vision.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that since it is agreed that knowledge can relate to God, vision too can be permissible. The conclusion is about rational possibility, not actual occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد سلك الأستاذ أبو إسحاق طريقة قريبة من هذه فقال: الرؤية معنى لا تؤثر في المرئي ولا تتأثر منه، فإن حكمه حكم العلم بخلاف سائر الحواس فإنها تؤثر وتتأثر، وإنما يلزم الاستحالة فيه أن لو تأثرت الرؤية من المرئي أو تأثر المرئي من الرؤية، وكل ما هذا سبيله فهو جائز التعلق بالقديم والحادث، وكل مؤثر ومتأثر فهو مستحيل عندنا كما هو مستحيل عندكم، ولا كلفة في هذه الطريقة إلا إثبات معنى في البصر لا يؤثر ولا يتأثر، وقد أثبتنا من قبل أن الإدراك البصري لا يستدعي اتصال شعاع بالمرئي ولا انفصال شيء من الرائي بطل الوجهان انتفى التأثير والتأثر وصار المعنى كالعلم أو هو من جنس العلم، وقد تقرر الاتفاق على جواز تعلق العلم به وهذا نهاية ما قيل في إثبات الجواز من جهة العقل.
And the master Abu Ishaq followed an approach close to this and said: Vision is a meaning that does not affect the seen nor is affected by it, for its ruling is the ruling of knowledge unlike the other senses, for they affect and are affected. And impossibility is only necessary in it if vision were affected by the seen or the seen were affected by vision. And everything that is such is permissible to be related to the eternal and the temporal. And every affecter and affected is impossible according to us just as it is impossible according to you. And there is no burden in this approach except establishing a meaning in the eye that does not affect nor is affected. And we have established before that visual perception does not require the connection of a ray to the seen nor the detachment of something from the seer. So both aspects are invalid, effect and being affected are negated, and the meaning becomes like knowledge or is of the genus of knowledge. And it has been established by agreement the permissibility of knowledge being related to Him, and this is the utmost that has been said in establishing permissibility from the aspect of reason.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for the permissibility of seeing God (ru'ya). The author, Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, argues that vision is a meaning (ma'na) that does not affect the object seen nor is affected by it, precisely like knowledge. Unlike other senses (e.g., touch, smell) which involve physical interaction and thus cannot be attributed to God due to His transcendence, vision can be conceived without such interaction. The key point is that impossibility only arises if vision involved effect or being affected. Since the author has already established that visual perception does not require a ray or any physical connection, the meaning of vision is akin to knowledge. Since it is agreed that knowledge can relate to God (i.e., He knows all things), vision too can be permissible. Thus, the argument establishes the rational possibility (jawaz) of seeing God.

Key Terms
رؤية (vision)
معنى (meaning)
علم (knowledge)
تأثير (effect)
تأثر (being affected)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision necessarily involves physical interaction (e.g., rays emitting from the eye).
Correction: Abu Ishaq argues that vision is a meaning like knowledge, without effect or being affected, so it is not inherently physical.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Ishaq's argument, why is vision of God possible?
A) Because God has a physical form
B) Because vision does not affect nor is affected, like knowledge
C) Because vision requires a ray
D) Because all senses are alike

Key Takeaway
Vision is rationally permissible for God because it is a meaning like knowledge, devoid of physical effect or being affected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Third Mu'tazila Objection: Vision Does Not Apply to Bare Substance or Accident
The Mu'tazila object that vision does not apply to bare substance or every accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third Mu'tazila objection to the Ash'ari proof for the possibility of seeing Allah, focusing on the claim that vision does not apply to bare substance or all accidents.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila challenge the Ash'ari generalization that vision relates to all substances and accidents.

This lesson presents the third of several objections raised by the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari argument for the possibility of seeing Allah. The Mu'tazila argue that the Ash'aris have overgeneralized from sensory experience, since vision does not actually apply to bare substances or to every accident. The passage is incomplete, but we can still extract the core criticism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation to grasp the objection as presented.
Note that the Arabic cuts off mid-sentence, so the full objection is not preserved.
2
Identify the two parts of the objection
The objection has two parts: first, concerning bare substance (جوهر مجرد) and bare body; second, concerning accident (عرض). Understand each part separately.
For the first part, the Mu'tazila argue that vision cannot relate to a substance stripped of color, because such a thing is impossible. For the second, they note that not every accident is visible.
3
Reflect on the challenge to the Ash'ari analogy
Consider how this objection undermines the Ash'ari proof from worldly vision. If vision does not actually apply to bare substances or all accidents, the analogy between seeing created things and seeing Allah is weakened.

This passage presents the third Mu'tazila objection, which is incomplete in the available text. However, they point out that vision does not actually apply to a bare substance or a bare body that is devoid of color, because such a thing is impossible in reality (substances always have accidents like color). Similarly, not every accident is visible (e.g., sounds, smells). Therefore, the Ash'ari generalisation is not accurate, and their argument would only work if vision actually related to all substances and accidents in their bare state. The objection is cut off, but it challenges the premise of the Ash'ari analogy.

The Fourth Objection: Hudduth as a Qualifier for Vision
Objection using analogy of visible and known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that hudduth (origination) can be a valid qualifier for vision, based on the analogy between the visible and the known.

Core Idea
Hudduth can qualify vision just as existence qualifies knowledge.

The objector challenges the Ashʿarī view that only existence qualifies for vision. He points out that rational judgments sometimes attach to hudduth itself. Through a careful analogy, he argues that vision, like knowledge, can be conditioned by specific attributes such as hudduth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the objection's logic sink in.
Note the phrase 'وكما أن المرئي أخص من المعلوم' – this is the core analogy.
2
Understand the analogy
The objector compares the relationship between 'visible' and 'known' to that between 'existence' and 'hudduth'. Just as vision attaches to the known under the attribute of existence, it can attach to existence under the attribute of hudduth.
The visible is a subset of the known; similarly, hudduth is an attribute of existence.
3
Identify the implication
Recognize that if this analogy holds, then hudduth can indeed be a qualifier (musahhih) for vision, undermining the Ashʿarī position.
The objector extends this to other attributes like opposition or becoming.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتراض رابع : لم قلتم : إن الحدوث لا يكون مصححا وقد عرفتم أن من الأحكام العقلية ما يتعلق بالحدوث دون الوجود؟ وليس لقائل أن يقول : إن كونه مسبوقا بعدم ليس بمؤثر فإن معناه أنه وجود على صفة واعتبار، وكما أن المرئي أخص من المعلوم، والرؤية تتعلق بالمعلوم على صفة الوجود، كذلك نقول : تتعلق بالوجود على صفة الحدوث، وبالحادث على صفة المقابلة أو التكون، أو غير ذلك من الصفات والاعتبارات، وهذا السر وهو أن المنقسم ربما يورد أقسامًا وينفي بعض الأقسام مفردًا، وربما يكون الحكم معلقًا بمركب من القسمين لا بمفرد منهما فكما يجب نفي الأفراد من حيث هي أفراد فكذلك يجب نفي المركب من حيث هو مركب، وهذا مما أغفله المتكلم كثيرًا في مجاري تقسيماته، وهو واجب الرعاية جدا.
And a fourth objection: Why did you say that hudduth (origination) cannot be a qualifier (musahhih), while you know that among rational judgments there are those that attach to hudduth and not to existence? And no one can say that its being preceded by non-existence is ineffective, for its meaning is that it is existence qualified by an attribute and consideration. Just as the visible is more specific than the known, and vision attaches to the known by the attribute of existence, similarly we say: it attaches to existence by the attribute of hudduth, and to the originated by the attribute of opposition or becoming [al-takawwun], or other attributes and considerations. And this secret is that the divided thing may sometimes be presented in parts and some parts be denied individually, and sometimes the judgment may be attached to a composite of the two parts and not to each alone. So just as it is necessary to deny individuals insofar as they are individuals, so too it is necessary to deny the composite insofar as it is composite. And this is something the theologian (mutakallim) often neglects in his divisions, and it is highly necessary to observe.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth objection posed by an opponent against the Ashʿarī position that the qualifier (musahhih) for vision is mere existence. The objector argues that hudduth (origination) can also serve as a qualifier, noting that rational judgments sometimes attach to hudduth rather than existence. He then explains that being preceded by non-existence is not ineffective; rather, it means existence with a specific attribute. He draws an analogy: just as the visible is more specific than the known, and vision attaches to the known under the attribute of existence, so vision can attach to existence under the attribute of hudduth, or to the originated under the attribute of opposition or becoming. The objector then highlights a subtle point about division and composition: when something is divided into parts, one might deny some parts individually, but the judgment might actually attach to a composite of those parts. So denying the individual parts does not suffice; one must also deny the composite. The objector criticizes that theologians often neglect this nuance in their divisions.

Key Terms
الحدوث
المصحح
الرؤية
الوجود
المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that only existence qualifies for vision, and no other attribute can serve as a qualifier.
Correction: The objector argues that hudduth and other attributes can qualify vision, as shown by the analogy of the visible and the known.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth objection, why can hudduth be a valid qualifier for vision?
A) Because hudduth is independent of existence.
B) Because vision attaches to existence under the attribute of hudduth, just as the visible is a special case of the known.
C) Because hudduth is a rational judgment that overrides existence.
D) Because the opponent has already conceded this point.

Key Takeaway
The objector uses the analogy of the visible and the known to argue that hudduth can be a qualifier for vision.

?

The Principle of Composite Denial in Theological Divisions
Explanation of the rule about denying composites in divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subtle logical rule that when a judgment attaches to a composite of parts, denying the individual parts does not suffice; one must also deny the composite.

Core Idea
Denying parts individually does not deny the composite.

The objector deepens the critique by pointing out a common oversight in theological reasoning. He explains that when something is divided into parts, the judgment may be attached to the composite, not to the parts separately. Theologians often neglect this, but it is crucial to observe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the distinction between individual parts and the composite.
The key phrase is 'ربما يكون الحكم معلقًا بمركب من القسمين'.
2
Understand the rule about composites
The objector states that sometimes a judgment is attached to a combination of two parts, not to each part alone. Therefore, denying each part individually does not adequately address the composite judgment.
This is a logical subtlety often missed.
3
Reflect on its importance
Consider how this principle applies to the current debate: the opponent might think denying hudduth as a qualifier is enough, but the objector argues that the qualifier might be a composite of existence and hudduth.
The objector criticizes theologians for neglecting this in their divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتراض رابع : لم قلتم : إن الحدوث لا يكون مصححا وقد عرفتم أن من الأحكام العقلية ما يتعلق بالحدوث دون الوجود؟ وليس لقائل أن يقول : إن كونه مسبوقا بعدم ليس بمؤثر فإن معناه أنه وجود على صفة واعتبار، وكما أن المرئي أخص من المعلوم، والرؤية تتعلق بالمعلوم على صفة الوجود، كذلك نقول : تتعلق بالوجود على صفة الحدوث، وبالحادث على صفة المقابلة أو التكون، أو غير ذلك من الصفات والاعتبارات، وهذا السر وهو أن المنقسم ربما يورد أقسامًا وينفي بعض الأقسام مفردًا، وربما يكون الحكم معلقًا بمركب من القسمين لا بمفرد منهما فكما يجب نفي الأفراد من حيث هي أفراد فكذلك يجب نفي المركب من حيث هو مركب، وهذا مما أغفله المتكلم كثيرًا في مجاري تقسيماته، وهو واجب الرعاية جدا.
And a fourth objection: Why did you say that hudduth (origination) cannot be a qualifier (musahhih), while you know that among rational judgments there are those that attach to hudduth and not to existence? And no one can say that its being preceded by non-existence is ineffective, for its meaning is that it is existence qualified by an attribute and consideration. Just as the visible is more specific than the known, and vision attaches to the known by the attribute of existence, similarly we say: it attaches to existence by the attribute of hudduth, and to the originated by the attribute of opposition or becoming [al-takawwun], or other attributes and considerations. And this secret is that the divided thing may sometimes be presented in parts and some parts be denied individually, and sometimes the judgment may be attached to a composite of the two parts and not to each alone. So just as it is necessary to deny individuals insofar as they are individuals, so too it is necessary to deny the composite insofar as it is composite. And this is something the theologian (mutakallim) often neglects in his divisions, and it is highly necessary to observe.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth objection posed by an opponent against the Ashʿarī position that the qualifier (musahhih) for vision is mere existence. The objector argues that hudduth (origination) can also serve as a qualifier, noting that rational judgments sometimes attach to hudduth rather than existence. He then explains that being preceded by non-existence is not ineffective; rather, it means existence with a specific attribute. He draws an analogy: just as the visible is more specific than the known, and vision attaches to the known under the attribute of existence, so vision can attach to existence under the attribute of hudduth, or to the originated under the attribute of opposition or becoming. The objector then highlights a subtle point about division and composition: when something is divided into parts, one might deny some parts individually, but the judgment might actually attach to a composite of those parts. So denying the individual parts does not suffice; one must also deny the composite. The objector criticizes that theologians often neglect this nuance in their divisions.

Key Terms
المنقسم
أقسام
مركب
أفراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying all parts individually is sufficient to deny the composite judgment.
Correction: The objector explains that if the judgment attaches to the composite, one must deny the composite as such, not just its parts.

Checkpoint
What is the subtle point the objector makes about theological divisions?
A) Theologians always consider composites.
B) Denying individuals suffices even for composites.
C) When a judgment is attached to a composite, denying individuals is not enough.
D) Hudduth is not a qualifier.

Key Takeaway
In theological divisions, one must deny the composite as such, not just its parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you confidently defended a theological position by listing reasons against each individual argument of your opponent. Did you ever consider that your opponent's claim might be a composite — a combination of those reasons — and that by only denying the parts you left the composite untouched? How might this oversight have affected the truth of your defense? Will you examine your arguments more carefully from now on, especially regarding composite claims?
?
The passage warns that theologians often neglect this rule. If you are a student of theology, are you willing to admit that you might have fallen into this neglect? What change in your reasoning practice does this lesson demand of you today? Write down one specific step you will take to ensure you deny composites when necessary.
?
Your ultimate accountability is before Allah. If you present an argument that is logically flawed because you denied only parts, you might be misrepresenting the truth. Are you satisfied with that risk? What will you do to master this principle so that your defense of the faith is rigorous and complete?

Divine Attributes

The Fifth Objection: Generalizing the Qualifier to All Senses
Objection that if all senses perceive existence, then God would be perceived by all senses, which is shirk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma posed against the Ashʿarī claim that vision's qualifier is existence, and why the objection forces a choice between shirk and requiring a proof.

Core Idea
To generalize the qualifier to all senses implies shirk; to specify it to vision requires a proof.

The objector pushes the Ashʿarī onto the horns of a dilemma: either the qualifier for vision applies to all senses, making God perceptible by all—which is shirk—or it applies only to vision, but then a proof is needed. This lesson unpacks the objection and the attempted replies.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the passage and identify the two options presented: generalize the qualifier (existence) to all senses, or specify it only to vision.
The dilemma is a classic 'if-then-else' structure.
2
Examine the first horn
If they generalize, they commit to God being heard, smelled, tasted, and touched—which the objector calls 'great shirk'.
Why is this considered shirk? Because it implies divine attributes are like created things.
3
Examine the second horn
If they specify to vision, they must produce a proof for that specification. The passage then explores two attempted proofs.
The burden of proof is on the Ashʿarī to show why vision is special.
4
Analyze the rebuttals
The objector dismisses the first proof as question-begging and counters the second by distinguishing intellectual determination from sensory determination.
Pay attention to the concept of 'determination' (taʿyīn) and its two types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتراض خامس قد تحقق أن الرؤية من جملة الحواس الخمس أفتقولون أنها كلها تتعلق بالوجود والمصحح لها الوجود، أم الرؤية خاصة تتعلق بكل موجود، فإن عممتم الحكم فقد افتتحتم أمرًا وهو التزام كونه تعالى مسموعا مشمومًا مطعوماً ملموسًا وذلك شرك عظيم، وإن خصصتم الحكم بالرؤية وجب عليكم إظهار دليل التخصيص ، فإن قلتم : الدليل أنه لا يؤثر في المرئي ولا يتأثر منه ، فقيل فقيل : هو نفس المتنازع فيه، وإن قلتم : هو علم أو في حكم العلم إلا أنه يقتضي تعيين المدرك ، فيقال لكم التعيين قد يكون تعيين العقل، ولا شك أن الحس لا يستدعي ذلك التعيين فإن تعيين العقل كتخصيص العام، وتفصيل المجمل، وتقييد المطلق، وتحديد الشيء، ولا تختص أمثال هذه التعيينات بالرؤية والحس وإن قلتم التعيين تعيين الحس فهو يستدعي قبول الإشارة إليه، وذلك يقتضي حيزًا ومكانا وجهة ومقابلة، وهو محال.
And a fifth objection: It is established that vision is one of the five senses. Do you say that all of them attach to existence and the qualifier for them is existence, or is vision specifically attached to every existent? For if you generalize the judgment, you have initiated a matter—namely, committing to His being heard, smelled, tasted, and touched—and that is great shirk. And if you specify the judgment to vision, then you must produce a proof of specification. If you say: the proof is that it [vision] neither affects the visible nor is affected by it, then it is said: that is exactly the point of dispute. And if you say: it is knowledge or in the ruling of knowledge, except that it requires determination of the perceived object, then it is said to you: determination may be determination of the intellect. There is no doubt that sense does not necessitate that determination, for determination by the intellect is like specifying the general, detailing the ambiguous, restricting the absolute, and defining a thing. Such determinations are not specific to vision and sense. And if you say: determination is determination of sense, then it requires acceptance of pointing to it, and that necessitates a bounded location, a place, a direction, and opposition, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth objection against the Ashʿarī position that vision is attached to existence alone. The objector argues that since vision is one of the five senses, the Ashʿarī must either hold that all senses attach to existence (generalizing the qualifier) or that only vision is specific. If they generalize, then God would be perceptible by all senses—heard, smelled, tasted, touched—which is shirk. If they specify vision, they must provide a proof for that specification. The objector then considers two possible proofs they might offer: (1) that vision neither affects nor is affected by the visible—but that is exactly the point in dispute. (2) That vision is knowledge or like knowledge but requires determination of the perceived object. The objector replies that determination can be intellectual, not sensory, and such intellectual determination is not unique to vision. If they claim it is sensory determination, then that would require pointing, implying location and direction, which is impossible for God.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الحواس
الوجود
التخصيص
التعيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if existence is the qualifier for vision, then it must also be the qualifier for all senses, but the Ashʿarī do not claim that.
Correction: The Ashʿarī hold that vision is special; the objector is forcing them to justify that specificity.

Checkpoint
What is the main dilemma posed in the fifth objection?
A) If all senses perceive existence, God would be perceived by all senses, which is absurd; if only vision does, a proof of specification is needed.
B) The Ashʿarī cannot prove that vision attaches to existence.
C) Vision is not a sense but a form of knowledge.
D) The objector accepts that vision is special.

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection forces the Ashʿarī to either admit shirk or provide a valid proof for singling out vision among the senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that claiming God can be perceived by the senses is shirk. Have you ever considered God's transcendence so seriously that you avoid any mental image or sensory attribute? What subtle ways might you still allow your imagination to 'place' God somewhere, and how can you purify your belief today?
?
The objector demands a proof for specifying vision. In your own faith, what proofs do you rely on for the possibility of seeing God in the hereafter? Are they based on revelation alone or also on rational arguments like those discussed here? Are you content with your certainty, or is there a lingering doubt you need to address?
?
Ashʿarī theologians were forced to confront the implications of their claim. When you face a theological dilemma that could lead to shirk, do you honestly examine all options, or do you avoid the difficulty? What will you do with this lesson's challenge to ensure your beliefs are sound?
